                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Monodisperse FePt nanoparticles and ferromagnetic FePt nanocrystal superlattices</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Shouheng; Murray, C. B.; Weller, Dieter; Folks, Liesl; Moser, Andreas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, D. C.)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">287</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5460</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1989-1992</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0036-8075</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Synthesis of monodisperse iron-platinum (FePt) nanoparticles by redn. of platinum acetylacetonate and decompn. of iron pentacarbonyl in the presence of oleic acid and oleyl amine stabilizers is reported.  The FePt particle compn. is readily controlled, and the size is tunable from 3- to 10-nm diam. with a std. deviation of &lt;5%.  These nanoparticles self-assemble into three-dimensional superlattices.  Thermal annealing converts the internal particle structure from a chem. disordered fcc. phase to the chem. ordered face-centered tetragonal phase and transforms the nanoparticle superlattices into ferromagnetic nanocrystal assemblies.  These assemblies are chem. and mech. robust and can support high-d. magnetization reversal transitions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGorUvH7aQCwv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgeoqbn8CxStw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3cXhvF2rt7g%253D&amp;md5=768a2753fda79aefacd448a0c7a23766</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.49.4485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, C.                 10.1103/PhysRevB.49.4485</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D49%26publication_year%3D1994%26pages%3D4485-4493%26journal%3DPhys.%2BRev.%2BB%253A%2BCondens.%2BMatter%2BMater.%2BPhys.%26author%3DC.%2BH.%2BPark%26author%3DB.-H.%2BCheong%26author%3DK.-H.%2BLee%26author%3DK.%2BJ.%2BChang%26title%3DStructural%2Band%2BElectronic%2BProperties%2Bof%2BCubic%252C%2B2H%252C%2B4H%252C%2Band%2B6H%2BSiC%26doi%3D10.1103%252FPhysRevB.49.4485&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.8b07744&amp;doiOfLink=10.1103%2FPhysRevB.49.4485&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural and electronic properties of cubic, 2H, 4H, and 6H SiC</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Park, C. H.; Cheong, Byoung-Ho; Lee, Keun-Ho; Chang, K. J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Review B:  Condensed Matter and Materials Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1994</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4485-93</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRBMDO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0163-1829</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors study the structural and electronic properties of various polytypes of SiC through self-consistent ab initio pseudopotential calcns.  For the wurtzite (2H), 4H, and 6H structures, the equil. lattice consts. and bulk moduli are very similar to those for the cubic structure.  The energies calcd. for the polytypes considered here are very close to within 4.3 meV/atom, which may explain the polytypism of SiC.  The 4H structure is lowest in energy because of the attractive interactions between the alternating cubic and hexagonal stacking layers, while the wurtzite structure is most unstable among the polytypes.  The authors find the asym. charge distribution for a Si-C bond to be on the boundary sepg. the Zn-blende and wurtzite phases, which should be related to the polytypism of SiC.  In the hexagonal polytypes, the M conduction-band energy increases, while that of the K point decreases as the hexagonal close packing becomes more prominent.  Thus, the conduction-band min. state located at the X point for cubic SiC changes to the M point, and then to the K point for the 2H structure.  For the cubic structure, the d. of states near the conduction-band edge increases slowly with energy, while it shows very rapidly increasing behavior for the 6H polytype because its conduction-band edge states are flattened due to the band folding and the energy-increasing behavior of the M state when the hexagonal-close-packing nature is enhanced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrv2stxP8YgU7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgeoqbn8CxStw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2cXit1OnsLk%253D&amp;md5=9f322d00a0e45e3fbc2ab43b3a86e2be</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/s41557-018-0035-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, G.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High phase-purity 1T'-MoS2- and 1T'-MoSe2-layered crystals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Yifu; Nam, Gwang-Hyeon; He, Qiyuan; Wu, Xue-Jun; Zhang, Kang; Yang, Zhenzhong; Chen, Junze; Ma, Qinglang; Zhao, Meiting; Liu, Zhengqing; Ran, Fei-Rong; Wang, Xingzhi; Li, Hai; Huang, Xiao; Li, Bing; Xiong, Qihua; Zhang, Qing; Liu, Zheng; Gu, Lin; Du, Yonghua; Huang, Wei; Zhang, Hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Phase control plays an important role in the precise synthesis of inorg. materials, as the phase structure has a profound influence on properties such as cond. and chem. stability.  Phase-controlled prepn. has been challenging for the metallic-phase group-VI transition metal dichalcogenides (the transition metals are Mo and W, and the chalcogens are S, Se and Te), which show better performance in electrocatalysis than their semiconducting counterparts.  Here, we report the large-scale prepn. of micrometre-sized metallic-phase 1T'-MoX2 (X = S, Se)-layered bulk crystals in high purity.  We reveal that 1T'-MoS2 crystals feature a distorted octahedral coordination structure and are convertible to 2H-MoS2 following thermal annealing or laser irradn.  Electrochem. measurements show that the basal plane of 1T'-MoS2 is much more active than that of 2H-MoS2 for the electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution reaction in an acidic medium.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpYnxfdBpmaELVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLkoxrb-UXJw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXos1SmsbY%253D&amp;md5=bf0fb62cc464583d0d6f7862fb8ffd02</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b12715"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Luo, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of 4H/fcc Noble Multimetallic Nanoribbons for Electrocatalytic Hydrogen Evolution Reaction</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of 4H/fcc Noble Multimetallic Nanoribbons for Electrocatalytic Hydrogen Evolution Reaction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fan, Zhanxi; Luo, Zhimin; Huang, Xiao; Li, Bing; Chen, Ye; Wang, Jie; Hu, Yanling; Zhang, Hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Noble multimetallic nanomaterials, if only consisting of Au, Ag, Pt, and Pd, typically adopt the high-symmetry face-centered cubic (fcc) structure.  Here for the first time, by using the 4H/fcc Au@Ag nanoribbons (NRBs) as seeds, the synthesis is reported of 4H/fcc trimetallic Au@PdAg core-shell NRBs via the galvanic reaction method under ambient conditions.  Moreover, this strategy can also be used to synthesize 4H/fcc trimetallic Au@PtAg and quatermetallic Au@PtPdAg core-shell NRBs.  Impressively, for the first time, these alloy shells, i.e., PdAg, PtAg, and PtPdAg, epitaxially grown on the 4H/fcc Au core with novel 4H hexagonal phase were successfully synthesized.  Remarkably, the obtained 4H/fcc Au@PdAg NRBs exhibit excellent electrocatalytic activity toward the hydrogen evolution reaction, which is even quite close to that of the com. Pt black.  The findings here may provide a novel strategy for the crystal-structure-controlled synthesis of advanced functional noble multimetallic nanomaterials with various promising applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfGmD0oYlMOLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLkoxrb-UXJw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XltFeksw%253D%253D&amp;md5=bfac1017801dd3b2aa61cfbb1530710c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C5CS90037A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chhowalla, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Two-Dimensional Transition Metal Dichalcogenide (TMD) Nanosheets</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Progress, challenges, and opportunities in two-dimensional materials beyond graphene</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Butler, Sheneve Z.; Hollen, Shawna M.; Cao, Linyou; Cui, Yi; Gupta, Jay A.; Gutierrez, Humberto R.; Heinz, Tony F.; Hong, Seung Sae; Huang, Jiaxing; Ismach, Ariel F.; Johnston-Halperin, Ezekiel; Kuno, Masaru; Plashnitsa, Vladimir V.; Robinson, Richard D.; Ruoff, Rodney S.; Salahuddin, Sayeef; Shan, Jie; Shi, Li; Spencer, Michael G.; Terrones, Mauricio; Windl, Wolfgang; Goldberger, Joshua E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Graphene's success has shown that it is possible to create stable, single and few-atom-thick layers of van der Waals materials, and also that these materials can exhibit fascinating and technol. useful properties.  Here, the authors review the state-of-the-art of 2-dimensional materials beyond graphene.  Initially, the authors outline the different chem. classes of 2-dimensional materials and discuss the various strategies to prep. single-layer, few-layer, and multilayer assembly materials in soln., on substrates, and on the wafer scale.  Addnl., the authors present an exptl. guide for identifying and characterizing single-layer-thick materials, as well as outlining emerging techniques that yield both local and global information.  They describe the differences that occur in the electronic structure between the bulk and the single layer and discuss various methods of tuning their electronic properties by manipulating the surface.  Finally, they highlight the properties and advantages of single-, few-, and many-layer 2-dimensional materials in field-effect transistors, spin- and valley-tronics, thermoelecs., and topol. insulators, among many other applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqDg1ruyhmhk7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liLkoxrb-UXJw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXjs1yitbs%253D&amp;md5=8dea00dca2666d030b5359758c72199f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.5b00287"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Eng, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered inorg. systems exhibit many technol. important and scientifically interesting properties, often linked to their anisotropy.  A typical example is graphite, which consists of stacked graphene monolayers.  Several other materials possess a layered structure similar to graphite and have gained enormous attention in recent years include transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs), metal carbides, layered oxides, black phosphorus, and layered structure halides.  From this wide spectra of layered inorg. materials, the layered transition-metal dichalcogenides which have the general chem. formula MX2, where M is a transition metal (i.e., Mo, W, Re) and X is a chalcogen, generally S, Se, or Te, exhibit the most favorable electrochem. properties.  Studies of the electrochem. behavior of TMDs potentially advance their development for various electrochem. applications.  While there is a large vol. of literature esp. dedicated to reviewing the applications of TMDs, particularly useful for hydrogen prodn., supercapacitors, or batteries, we wish to focus our discussion here rather on their fundamental electrochem. properties.  Focusing our attention on MoS2,WS2, MoSe2, and WSe2 as the most researched TMD materials, we will discuss, in particular, their inherent electrochem., highlighting the often overlooked fact that such materials possess important and well-defined redox properties.  We then move on to discuss the use of these TMDs as electrode-modifier materials for important electrochem. applications such as (I) the probe and detection of redox-active mols. in soln., (II)electrocatalytic hydrogen generation,(III)the oxygen redn. reaction, and (IV) the charge storage capabilities for the fabrication of supercapacitors.  A long with providing useful direct comparisons of MoS2, WS2, MoSe2, and WSe2, which are generally missing in the literature, we also evaluate the emerging trends in research endeavored to enhance both the electrochem. and the catalytic properties of TMDs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0gmJzI_vsUbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liySrVR8u6Epg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsF2ltLrL&amp;md5=8d54a0f886b36a50b0aee6b35650e228</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1126/science.1194975"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Coleman, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The magnetic and electronic properties of MoS2 nanoribbons with zigzag and armchair edges are investigated using LSDA-DFT.  We found that the properties of the nanoribbons are very different from bulk MoS2 due to edge states.  Armchair nanoribbons could be metallic and exhibit a magnetic moment; however, when passivating with hydrogen, they become semiconducting.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin two-dimensional nanosheets of layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) are fundamentally and technologically intriguing.  In contrast to the graphene sheet, they are chemically versatile.  Mono- or few-layered TMDs - obtained either through exfoliation of bulk materials or bottom-up syntheses - are direct-gap semiconductors whose bandgap energy, as well as carrier type (n- or p-type), varies between compounds depending on their composition, structure and dimensionality.  In this Review, we describe how the tunable electronic structure of TMDs makes them attractive for a variety of applications.  They have been investigated as chemically active electrocatalysts for hydrogen evolution and hydrosulfurization, as well as electrically active materials in opto-electronics.  Their morphologies and properties are also useful for energy storage applications such as electrodes for Li-ion batteries and supercapacitors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcTRX3K9n3xrikBuFdjgVNKSfW6udTcc2eaLZfwXP1zyfbntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC3svotVamsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=3dbc30db63b6a32231ecf5cae8a71cd9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3439"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kibsgaard, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Reinecke, B.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering the surface structure of MoS2 to preferentially expose active edge sites for electrocatalysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kibsgaard, Jakob; Chen, Zhebo; Reinecke, Benjamin N.; Jaramillo, Thomas F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Controlling surface structure at the at. scale is paramount to developing effective catalysts.  For example, the edge sites of MoS2 are highly catalytically active and are thus preferred at the catalyst surface over MoS2 basal planes, which are inert.  However, thermodn. favors the presence of the basal plane, limiting the no. of active sites at the surface.  Herein, we engineer the surface structure of MoS2 to preferentially expose edge sites to effect improved catalysis by successfully synthesizing contiguous large-area thin films of a highly ordered double-gyroid MoS2 bicontinuous network with nanoscaled pores.  The high surface curvature of this catalyst mesostructure exposes a large fraction of edge sites, which, along with its high surface area, leads to excellent activity for electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution.  This work elucidates how morphol. control of materials at the nanoscale can significantly impact the surface structure at the at. scale, enabling new opportunities for enhancing surface properties for catalysis and other important technol. applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpzin8Ebl6IV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljGvkBiq9bnpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsVKisbfJ&amp;md5=7308b67fbbae7801cc75bb792c74d222</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/C4CS00276H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuc, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Heine, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Electronic Structure Calculations of Two-Dimensional Transition-Metal Dichalcogenides in the Presence of External Electric and Magnetic Fields</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The electronic structure calculations of two-dimensional transition-metal dichalcogenides in the presence of external electric and magnetic fields</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kuc, Agnieszka; Heine, Thomas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition-metal dichalcogenides TX2 (T = W, Mo; X = S, Se, Te) are layered materials that are available in ultrathin forms such as mono-, bi- and multilayers, which are commonly known as two-dimensional materials.  They have an intrinsic band gap in the range of some 500 meV to 2 eV, depending on the compn. and no. of layers, and giant intrinsic spin-orbit splittings for odd layer nos., and, in conjunction with their high chem. and mech. stability, they qualify as candidate materials for two-dimensional flexible electronics and spintronics.  The electronic structure of each TX2 material is very sensitive to external factors, in particular towards elec. and magnetic fields.  A perpendicular elec. field reduces the band gap, and for some structures semiconductor-metal transitions could be possible.  Moreover, the elec. field triggers the spin-orbit splitting for bilayers.  A magnetic field applied normal to the layers causes the Hall effect, which in some cases may result in a quantum (spin) Hall effect and thus in magnetic switches.  Finally, we discuss how valleytronics is possible in these materials by selective interaction of electrons in the different valleys using polarized light.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqZ57usKjm3mbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljGvkBiq9bnpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFegsbnJ&amp;md5=50f8b1b35df332086982d1dd9d07873e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201104798"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The role of electronic coupling between substrate and 2D MoS2 nanosheets in electrocatalytic production of hydrogen</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Voiry, Damien; Fullon, Raymond; Yang, Jieun; Castro e Silva, Cecilia de Carvalho; Kappera, Rajesh; Bozkurt, Ibrahim; Kaplan, Daniel; Lagos, Maureen J.; Batson, Philip E.; Gupta, Gautam; Mohite, Aditya D.; Dong, Liang; Er, Dequan; Shenoy, Vivek B.; Asefa, Tewodros; Chhowalla, Manish</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The excellent catalytic activity of metallic MoS2 edges for the hydrogen evolution reaction (HER) has led to substantial efforts towards increasing the edge concn.  The 2H basal plane is less active for the HER because it is less conducting and therefore possesses less efficient charge transfer kinetics.  Here we show that the activity of the 2H basal planes of monolayer MoS2 nanosheets can be made comparable to state-of-the-art catalytic properties of metallic edges and the 1T phase by improving the elec. coupling between the substrate and the catalyst so that electron injection from the electrode and transport to the catalyst active site is facilitated.  Phase-engineered low-resistance contacts on monolayer 2H-phase MoS2 basal plane lead to higher efficiency of charge injection in the nanosheets so that its intrinsic activity towards the HER can be measured.  We demonstrate that onset potentials and Tafel slopes of â¼-0.1 V and â¼50 mV per decade can be achieved from 2H-phase catalysts where only the basal plane is exposed.  We show that efficient charge injection and the presence of naturally occurring sulfur vacancies are responsible for the obsd. increase in catalytic activity of the 2H basal plane.  Our results provide new insights into the role of contact resistance and charge transport on the performance of two-dimensional MoS2 nanosheet catalysts for the HER.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqB5a1jVtnaO7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljGvkBiq9bnpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVWns7rO&amp;md5=d751b3d13813556df3bab497254c6e84</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.7b08682"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chiu, M.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Selectively Plasmon-Enhanced Second-Harmonic Generation from Monolayer Tungsten Diselenide on Flexible Substrates</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Zhuo; Dong, Zhaogang; Zhu, Hai; Jin, Lei; Chiu, Ming-Hui; Li, Lain-Jong; Xu, Qing-Hua; Eda, Goki; Maier, Stefan A.; Wee, Andrew T. S.; Qiu, Cheng-Wei; Yang, Joel K. W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Monolayer 2-dimensional transition metal dichalcogenides (2D TMDCs) exhibit promising characteristics in miniaturized nonlinear optical frequency converters, due to their inversion asymmetry and large 2nd-order nonlinear susceptibility.  These materials usually have very short light interaction lengths with the pump laser because they are atomically thin, such that 2nd-harmonic generation (SHG) is generally inefficient.  The authors fabricate a judiciously structured 150 nm-thick planar surface consisting of monolayer WSe2 and sub-20 nm-wide Au trenches on flexible substrates, reporting â¼7000-fold SHG enhancement without peak broadening or background in the spectra as compared to WSe2 on as-grown Al2O3 substrates.  The proof-of-concept expt. yields effective 2nd-order nonlinear susceptibility of 2.1 Ã 104 pm/V.  Three orders of magnitude enhancement is maintained with pump wavelength ranging 800-900 nm, breaking the limitation of narrow pump wavelength range for cavity-enhanced SHG.  SHG amplitude can be dynamically controlled via selective excitation of the lateral gap plasmon by rotating the laser polarization.  Such a fully open, flat, and ultrathin profile enables a great variety of functional samples with high SHG from 1 patterned Si substrate, favoring scalable prodn. of nonlinear converters.  The surface accessibility also enables integration with other optical components for information processing in an ultrathin and flexible form.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolfhkyAF4UUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lji6-NGFGVFgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXjsVOhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=cdbf1bb0eb478e8f5d16d470b842e3f9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201302685"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controllable Disorder Engineering in Oxygen-Incorporated MoS2 Ultrathin Nanosheets for Efficient Hydrogen Evolution</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xie, Junfeng; Zhang, Jiajia; Li, Shuang; Grote, Fabian; Zhang, Xiaodong; Zhang, Hao; Wang, Ruoxing; Lei, Yong; Pan, Bicai; Xie, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) has emerged as a promising electrocatalyst for catalyzing protons to hydrogen via the so-called hydrogen evolution reaction (HER).  In order to enhance the HER activity, tremendous effort has been made to engineer MoS2 catalysts with either more active sites or higher cond.  However, at present, synergistically structural and electronic modulations for HER still remain challenging.  In this work, synergistic regulations are demonstrated of both structural and electronic benefits by controllable disorder engineering and simultaneous oxygen incorporation in MoS2 catalysts, leading to the dramatically enhanced HER activity.  The disordered structure can offer abundant unsatd. sulfur atoms as active sites for HER, while the oxygen incorporation can effectively regulate the electronic structure and further improve the intrinsic cond.  By means of controllable disorder engineering and oxygen incorporation, an optimized catalyst with a moderate degree of disorder was developed, exhibiting superior activity for electrocatalytic hydrogen evolution.  In general, the optimized catalyst exhibits onset overpotential as low as 120 mV, accompanied by extremely large cathodic c.d. and excellent stability.  This work will pave a new pathway for improving the electrocatalytic activity by synergistically structural and electronic modulations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr0zM1-DzoMdrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lji6-NGFGVFgA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslaksb7L&amp;md5=0ce1ea2fd4b02d6832e1783f39e59a37</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3700"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Voiry, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamaguchi, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Silva, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Alves, D.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhanced catalytic activity in strained chemically exfoliated WS2 nanosheets for hydrogen evolution</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Voiry, Damien; Yamaguchi, Hisato; Li, Junwen; Silva, Rafael; Alves, Diego C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Efficient evolution of hydrogen through water electrocatalysis at low overpotentials holds tremendous promise for clean energy.  Hydrogen evolution can be easily achieved by electrolysis at large potentials that can be lowered with expensive platinum-based catalysts.  Replacement of Pt with inexpensive, earth-abundant electrocatalysts would be significantly beneficial for clean and efficient hydrogen evolution.  To this end, promising results have been reported using 2H (trigonal prismatic) XS2 (where X = Mo or W) nanoparticles with a high concn. of metallic edges.  The key challenges for XS2 are increasing the no. and catalytic activity of active sites.  Here we report monolayered nanosheets of chem. exfoliated WS2 as efficient catalysts for hydrogen evolution with very low overpotentials.  Analyses indicate that the enhanced electrocatalytic activity of WS2 is assocd. with the high concn. of the strained metallic 1T (octahedral) phase in the as-exfoliated nanosheets.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phase-engineered low-resistance contacts for ultrathin MoS2 transistors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kappera, Rajesh; Voiry, Damien; Yalcin, Sibel Ebru; Branch, Brittany; Gupta, Gautam; Mohite, Aditya D.; Chhowalla, Manish</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) has emerged as an interesting layered semiconductor because of its finite energy bandgap and the absence of dangling bonds.  However, metals deposited on the semiconducting 2H phase usually form high-resistance (0.7 kÎ© Î¼m-10 kÎ© Î¼m) contacts, leading to Schottky-limited transport.  The metallic 1T phase of MoS2 can be locally induced on semiconducting 2H phase nanosheets, thus decreasing contact resistances to 200-300 Î© Î¼m at zero gate bias.  Field-effect transistors (FETs) with 1T phase electrodes fabricated and tested in air exhibit mobility values of â¼50 cm2 V-1 s-1, subthreshold swing values &lt;100 mV per decade, on/off ratios of &gt;107, drive currents approaching â¼100 Î¼A Î¼m-1, and excellent current satn.  The deposition of different metals has limited influence on the FET performance, suggesting that the 1T/2H interface controls carrier injection into the channel.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Phase transitions can be used to alter the properties of a material without adding any addnl. atoms and are therefore of significant technol. value. Here, we show that in situ scanning TEM can be used to follow the structural transformation between semiconducting (2H) and metallic (1T) phases in single-layered MoS2, with at. resoln.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrical Transport Properties of Polymorphic MoS2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Jun Suk; Kim, Jaesu; Zhao, Jiong; Kim, Sungho; Lee, Jin Hee; Jin, Youngjo; Choi, Homin; Moon, Byoung Hee; Bae, Jung Jun; Lee, Young Hee; Lim, Seong Chu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The engineering of polymorphs in two-dimensional layered materials has recently attracted significant interest.  Although the semiconducting (2H) and metallic (1T) phases are known to be stable in thin-film MoTe2, semiconducting 2H-MoS2 is locally converted into metallic 1T-MoS2 through chem. lithiation.  The authors describe the observation of the 2H, 1T, and 1T' phases coexisting in Li-treated MoS2, which result in unusual transport phenomena.  Although multiphase MoS2 shows no transistor-gating response, the channel resistance decreases in proportion to the temp., similar to the behavior of a typical semiconductor.  TEM images clearly show that the 1T and 1T' phases are randomly distributed and intervened with 2H-MoS2, which is referred to as the 1T and 1T' puddling phenomenon.  The resistance curve fits well with 2-dimensional-variable range-hopping transport behavior, where electrons hop over 1T domains that are bounded by semiconducting 2H phases.  However, near 30 K, electrons hop over charge puddles.  The large temp. coeff. of resistance (TCR) of multiphase MoS2, -2.0 Ã 10-2 K-1 at 300 K, allows for efficient IR detection at room temp. by the photothermal effect.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7YWuhdw9T8rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgyVA8BQr5sIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtFGmtbzI&amp;md5=e27f15b2441ab8d627ad8f360e3cfbb6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b04260"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 is a promising electrode material for energy storage.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultrathin molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) has emerged as an interesting layered semiconductor because of its finite energy bandgap and the absence of dangling bonds.  However, metals deposited on the semiconducting 2H phase usually form high-resistance (0.7 kÎ© Î¼m-10 kÎ© Î¼m) contacts, leading to Schottky-limited transport.  The metallic 1T phase of MoS2 can be locally induced on semiconducting 2H phase nanosheets, thus decreasing contact resistances to 200-300 Î© Î¼m at zero gate bias.  Field-effect transistors (FETs) with 1T phase electrodes fabricated and tested in air exhibit mobility values of â¼50 cm2 V-1 s-1, subthreshold swing values &lt;100 mV per decade, on/off ratios of &gt;107, drive currents approaching â¼100 Î¼A Î¼m-1, and excellent current satn.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Superconducting layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) stand out among other superconductors due to the tunable nature of the superconducting transition, coexistence with other collective electronic excitations (charge d. waves), and strong intrinsic spin-orbit coupling.  Molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) is the most studied representative of this family of materials, esp. since the recent demonstration of the possibility to tune its crit. temp., Tc, by elec.-field doping.  However, just one of its polymorphs, band-insulator 2H-MoS2, has so far been explored for its potential to host supercond.  The authors have studied the possibility to induce supercond. in metallic polytypes, 1T- and 1T'-MoS2, by potassium (K) intercalation.  At doping levels significantly higher than that required to induce supercond. in 2H-MoS2, both 1T and 1T' phases become superconducting with Tc = 2.8 and 4.6 K, resp.  Unusually, K intercalation in this case is responsible both for the structural and superconducting phase transitions.  By adding new members to the family of superconducting TMDs, the authors' findings open the way to further manipulate and enhance the electronic properties of these technol. important materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKesWcf6mq3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgL36nhUnB3Lg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvFWrsLvN&amp;md5=67f9751fb141d13d9168adc481095e14</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.44.3955"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">JimÃ©nez

    </div><div class="casAbstract">A valence-force-field model was used to study the lattice dynamics of MoS2 single mol. layers.  A comparison between the room-temp. Raman spectra of aq. suspensions and the calcd. phonon-dispersion curves for single layers of MoS2 with trigonal prism and octahedral coordination indicates that MoS2 single layers adopt a structure in which the Mo atoms are octahedrally coordinated, as opposed to bulk MoS2 where the trigonal prism coordination is usually found.  Besides the zone-center modes, strong Raman peaks were obsd. at 156, 226, and 333 cm-1.  The presence of these peaks is attributed to a zone-folding mechanism resulting from the formation of a 2a0 Ã a0 superlattice in the single layers of MoS2.  The superlattice is, in turn, believed to correspond to the basal-plane at. arrangement of a distorted octahedral structure.  Lattice-dynamics calcns. based on an octahedrally coordinated structure with a significant metal-metal interaction yield good agreement with the obsd. frequencies.  When the single mol. layers restack and the S-S interaction between the layers is recovered, the layers convert back to the trigonal prismatic configuration of cryst.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  1T MoS2 is metallic octahedral type MoS2.  Compared to the most stable and widely existing triangle prismatic (2H) phase of MoS2, 1T MoS2 possesses distinct electrochem. and electronic properties, such as a high cond., abundant active sites both at the edges and on the basal plane for electrochem. catalysis, and enlarged interlayer distance.  Due to these merits, the last few years have witnessed a growing amt. of interest in both synthesis and application of this unique material.  So far, a series of methods have been developed to obtain 1T rich or 1T contg. MoS2 and the applications of 1T MoS2 have covered a wide range of areas from electrochem. reactions, photoelectrocatalysis and photodetectors to photothermal agents, energy storage devices, and biosensors.  Here, to obtain a hint of the key reasons for the formation of 1T structured MoS2 and instruct the exploration of more desirable new approaches, the means for fabricating 1T MoS2 that have been reported are classified and compared with a focus on the detailed procedures and the underlying mechanisms.  A full image of the explored applications with 1T MoS2 is provided with an emphasis on the relation between the properties of 1T MoS2 and the performance and a hope to inspire the development of other potential applications for 1T MoS2. Here the electrochem. dynamic process of MoS2 nanosheets upon lithium intercalation has been systematically investigated by in situ high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy.  The results indicate that the lithiated MoS2 undergoes a trigonal prismatic (2H)-octahedral (1T) phase transition with a lithium ion occupying the interlayer S-S tetrahedron site in the 1T-LiMoS2.  A pseudoperiodic structural modulation composed of polytype superlattices is also revealed as a consequence of the electron-lattice interaction.  Furthermore, the shear mechanism of the 2H-1T phase transition has been confirmed by probing the dynamic phase boundary movement.  The in situ real-time characterization at at. scale provides a great leap forward in the fundamental understanding of the lithium ion storage mechanism in MoS2, which should be also of help for other transition metal dichalcogenides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqJH94MWgtfEbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgppsDoTUry6g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXmtVentr0%253D&amp;md5=4b4a591d9c6b9680e1f85879476498ce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms3995"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tai Nai, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suk Shin, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Young Jeong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ping Loh, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Yield Exfoliation of Two-Dimensional Chalcogenides Using Sodium Naphthalenide</span>. <i>Nat.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition-metal dichalcogenides like molybdenum disulphide have attracted great interest as two-dimensional materials beyond graphene due to their unique electronic and optical properties.  Solution-phase processes can be a viable method for producing printable single-layer chalcogenides.  Molybdenum disulphide can be exfoliated into monolayer flakes using organolithium reduction chemistry; unfortunately, the method is hampered by low yield, submicron flake size and long lithiation time.  Here we report a high-yield exfoliation process using lithium, potassium and sodium naphthalenide where an intermediate ternary Li(x)MX(n) crystalline phase (X=selenium, sulphur, and so on) is produced.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Visibility of dichalcogenide nanolayers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Benameur, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Dichalcogcnides with the common formula MX2 are layered materials with elec. properties that range from semiconducting to superconducting.  Here, we describe optimal imaging conditions for the optical detection of ultrathin, two-dimensional dichalcogenide nanocrystals contg. single, double and triple layers of MoS2, WSe2 and NbSe2.  A simple optical model is used to calc. the contrast for nanolayers deposited on wafers with varying thicknesses of SiO2.  The model is extended for imaging using the green channel of a video camera.  Using AFM and optical imaging we confirm that single layers of MoS2 and VSe2 can be detected on 90 and 270 nm SiO2 using optical means.  By measuring contrast under broadband green illumination we are also able to distinguish between nanostructures contg. single, double and triple layers of MoS2 and WSe2.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Frictional characteristics of atomically thin sheets</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Changgu; Li, Qunyang; Kalb, William; Liu, Xin-Zhou; Berger, Helmuth; Carpick, Robert W.; Hone, James</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Using friction force microscopy, we compared the nanoscale frictional characteristics of atomically thin sheets of graphene, MoS2, NbSe2, and hexagonal BN exfoliated onto a weakly adherent substrate (Si oxide) to those of their bulk counterparts.  Measurements down to single at. sheets revealed that friction monotonically increased as the no. of layers decreased for all 4 materials.  Suspended graphene membranes showed the same trend, but binding the graphene strongly to a mica surface suppressed the trend.  Tip-sample adhesion forces were indistinguishable for all thicknesses and substrate arrangements.  Both graphene and MoS2 exhibited at. lattice stick-slip friction, with the thinnest sheets possessing a sliding-length-dependent increase in static friction.  These observations, coupled with finite element modeling, suggest that the trend arises from the thinner sheets' increased susceptibility to out-of-plane elastic deformation.  The generality of the results indicates that this may be a universal characteristic of nanoscale friction for atomically thin materials weakly bound to substrates.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrSByPO_LwkErVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhR2FY5_Z6Uww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXjvFKlsbk%253D&amp;md5=44b902089d28b318c07f066dabd2327a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201401802"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Najmaei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Halas, N.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors have demonstrated that hot electrons generated by plasmonic nanoparticles deposited on a MoS2 monolayer can induce a transient reversible 2H-to-1T phase transition in the layer.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Molybdenum disulfide is a layered transition metal dichalcogenide that has recently raised considerable interest due to its unique semiconducting and opto-electronic properties.  Although several theor. studies have suggested an electronic phase transition in molybdenum disulfide, there has been a lack of exptl. evidence.  Here we report comprehensive studies on the pressure-dependent electronic, vibrational, optical and structural properties of multilayered molybdenum disulfide up to 35 GPa.  Our exptl. results reveal a structural lattice distortion followed by an electronic transition from a semiconducting to metallic state at â¼19 GPa, which is confirmed by ab initio calcns.  The metalization arises from the overlap of the valance and conduction bands owing to sulfur-sulfur interactions as the interlayer spacing reduces.  The electronic transition affords modulation of the opto-electronic gain in molybdenum disulfide.  This pressure-tuned behavior can enable the development of novel devices with multiple phenomena involving the strong coupling of the mech., elec. and optical properties of layered nanomaterials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr1gyzWRtO6FrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhR2FY5_Z6Uww"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXksVCis7c%253D&amp;md5=599ab9be2060f9ee7c0707151ad67a8a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5214"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duerloo, K. Here we discover that mech. deformations provide a route to switching thermodn. stability between a semiconducting and a metallic crystal structure in these monolayer materials. Recovery of the semiconducting properties typically involves heating of the chem. exfoliated sheets at elevated temps.  Therefore, it was largely limited to sheets deposited on solid substrates. This process connects the scalability of chem. exfoliation with the simplicity of soln. processing, ultimately enabling a facile method for tuning the metal to semiconductor transitions of MoS2 and WS2 within a liq. medium.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrX_jQEjan31rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgEKfJcXeyI9g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlyrs70%253D&amp;md5=477bf7e593bbf5e7d089b71e295b04fd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1002/adma.200306136"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Magnetic-Field-Induced Growth of Single-Crystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Nanowires</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis and assembly of nanomaterials under magnetic fields</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Lin; Zhang, Ruirui; Chen, Qianwang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Traditionally, magnetic field has long been regarded as an important means for studying the magnetic properties of materials.  With the development of synthesis and assembly methods, magnetic field, similar to conventional reaction conditions such as temp., pressure, and surfactant, has been developed as a new parameter for synthesizing and assembling special structures.  To date, magnetic fields have been widely employed for materials synthesis and assembly of one-dimensional (1D), two-dimensional (2D) or three-dimensional (3D) aggregates.  In this review, we aim to provide a summary on the applications of magnetic fields in this area.  Overall, the objectives of this review are: (1) to theor. discuss several factors that refer to magnetic field effects (MFEs); (2) to review the magnetic-field-induced synthesis of nanomaterials; the 1D structure of various nanomaterials, such as metal oxides/sulfide, metals, alloys, and carbon, will be described in detail.  Moreover, the MFEs on spin states of ions, magnetic domain and product phase distribution will be also involved; (3) to review the alignment of carbon nanotubes, assembly of magnetic nanomaterials and photonic crystals with the help of magnetic fields; and (4) to sketch the future opportunities that magnetic fields can face in the area of materials synthesis and assembly.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqvQYAEZukm67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgEKfJcXeyI9g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhs1OhtLbK&amp;md5=73d37b99ef2e1f50f1a8454cd68a58ea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1038/349770a0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">De

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Microrods of the ferrosulfide minerals greigite (Fe3S4) and marcasite (FeS2) are selectively synthesized by an in situ magnetic-field-assisted hydrothermal route.  Each complex microrod is composed of fine building blocks with different shapes.  The unique magnetic properties of the microrods and elec. performance of a single microrod are studied.  The results demonstrate that the magnetic properties of the ferrosulfide minerals are strongly related to their corresponding microstructures.  The value of the low-temp. transition increases as the greigite component in the product decreases.  The combination of small-mol. sulfur precursors and an applied magnetic field makes possible the selective synthesis of ferrosulfide minerals with different phases and distinct microstructures, underlining the fact that the magnetic field can be a useful tool as well as an independent parameter for the phase-selective synthesis and self-assembly of inorg. building blocks in soln. chem.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoDovZ7HmBr4bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgEKfJcXeyI9g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XlsVKjsrc%253D&amp;md5=d6ddab5f955eacc1eb631cbc25d3381b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1021/cm702375e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Watanabe, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Magnetic-Field-Induced Synthesis of Magnetic Î³-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Nanotubes</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conditions and reasons for incoherent imaging in STEM</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hartel, P.; Rose, H.; Dinges, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ultramicroscopy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">63</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">93-114</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The origin of incoherent imaging in STEM was analyzed by studying the effects of the detector geometry and of the thermal vibrations of the atoms on the image formation.  The conditions for incoherent imaging are discussed.  In this case the Fourier transforms of the intensities at the exit plane of the object and at the image plane are linearly related with each other.  The corresponding transfer function coincides with the modulation transfer function for incoherent imaging in TEM.  By analyzing the properties of the degree of coherence, the reasons for the suppression of the interference terms are shown and detector arrangements are found which yield largely incoherent images.  The validity of the semianal. results for thin objects are also confirmed numerically for thick objects by a modified multislice algorithm.  With increasing object thickness the phonon scattered electrons dominate the image intensity.  Detector arrangements were found for which the elastic part of the image shows contrast reversal.  The dependence of the Z-contrast on the geometry of the annular detector and on the at. no. Z was studied.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOie_fYkf2BbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgk9cV7eb26sQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK28XlvVegu7o%253D&amp;md5=54c27f29768cd7d104b684dd36849f4a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201702998"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Geng, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mukhopadhyay, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Freestanding Metallic 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> with Dual Ion Diffusion Paths as High Rate Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">During Li deposition on single crystals of MoS2 at RT, Li is intercalated into MoS2 while two reactions take place: (a) an intercalation reaction according to the rigid band model, (b) an intercalation reaction accompanied by a phase transition 2Hâ1T.  The latter reaction dominates during Li intercalation across the van der Waals planes, while it is negligible and the first reaction prevails when (i) Li is intercalated along the van der Waals planes, (ii) the substrate temp. is elevated, and (iii) the Li deposition flux is relatively low.  A relation of the min. flux of Li deposition, at a certain temp., across the van der Waals planes of MoS2 necessary to prevent the phase transition and to dominate the rigid band intercalation reaction, is given as a function of the distance between successive mol. layers and the diffusion coeff. of Li.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrspwphP9Fx3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liQkLP627J7PA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2MXnvFWmu7c%253D&amp;md5=c2ad06ef753b42a0ad5242267224e3dd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit66" data-doi="10.1063/1.4905656"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhancement of Magnetism by Structural Phase Transition in MoS<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>Appl.                         cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enhancement of magnetism by structural phase transition in MoS2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Shiming; Qiao, Wen; He, Xueming; Guo, Xiaobing; Xi, Li; Zhong, Wei; Du, Youwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Physics Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The magnetic properties of 2H phase of MoS2 (2H-MoS2) and 1T phase of MoS2 (1T-MoS2) were studied both exptl. and theor.  Li intercalation method was used to prep. single-layer MoS2 sheets.  Pristine MoS2 (2H-MoS2) exhibited weak diamagnetism.  After exfoliating by Li intercalation, the crystal structure transformed from 2H to 1T phase, and the magnetism was significantly enhanced from diamagnetism to paramagnetism accordingly. Using crystal field theory and combining the results of 1st principle calcn., the enhanced magnetism can be attributed to the Mo atoms of 1T-MoS2.                         cas_cit68R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">68</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 Nanoflowers with Expanded Interlayers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Zhe; Wang, Lixiu; Zhang, Kai; Wang, Jianbin; Cheng, Fangyi; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayer spacing of the (002) plane were synthesized and used as high-performance anode in Na-ion batteries.  By controlling the cut-off voltage to the range of 0.4-3 V, an intercalation mechanism rather than a conversion reaction is taking place.  The MoS2 nanoflower electrode shows high discharge capacities of 350 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1, 300 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1, and 195 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1.  An initial capacity increase with cycling is caused by peeling off MoS2 layers, which produces more active sites for Na+ storage.  The stripping of MoS2 layers occurring in charge/discharge cycling contributes to the enhanced kinetics and low energy barrier for the intercalation of Na+ ions.  The electrochem. reaction is mainly controlled by the capacitive process, which facilitates the high-rate capability.  Therefore, MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayers hold promise for rechargeable Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0lywDrXEibbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liQkLP627J7PA"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tremendous efforts have been made to design the cathode of Li-S batteries to improve their energy d. and cycling life. Here we report a cathode architecture, which is constructed by burning lithium foils in a CS2 vapor.  The obtained structure features cryst. Li2S nanoparticles wrapped by few-layer graphene (Li2S@graphene nanocapsules).     </div><div class="casAbstract">QUANTUM ESPRESSO is an integrated suite of computer codes for electronic-structure calculations and materials modeling, based on density-functional theory, plane waves, and pseudopotentials (norm-conserving, ultrasoft, and projector-augmented wave).  The acronym ESPRESSO stands for opEn Source Package for Research in Electronic Structure, Simulation, and Optimization.  It is freely available to researchers around the world under the terms of the GNU General Public License.  QUANTUM ESPRESSO builds upon newly-restructured electronic-structure codes that have been developed and tested by some of the original authors of novel electronic-structure algorithms and applied in the last twenty years by some of the leading materials modeling groups worldwide.  Innovation and efficiency are still its main focus, with special attention paid to massively parallel architectures, and a great effort being devoted to user friendliness.  QUANTUM ESPRESSO is evolving towards a distribution of independent and interoperable codes in the spirit of an open-source project, where researchers active in the field of electronic-structure calculations are encouraged to participate in the project by contributing their own codes or by implementing their own ideas into existing codes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder Â®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcToTWfovXAMx7xanA7vODO6fW6udTcc2ea-BiIU87ibu7ntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>Â®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC3Mjltl2lug%253D%253D&amp;md5=da053fa748721b6b381051a20e7a7f53</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref73"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref73'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">73</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit73" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.41.7892"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vanderbilt, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Soft Self-Consistent Pseudopotentials in a Generalized Eigenvalue Formalism</span>. <i>Phys.                         cas_cit75R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">75</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Semiempirical GGA-type density functional constructed with a long-range dispersion correction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Grimme, Stefan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Computational Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2006</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1787-1799</span>CODEN:
